  104. As I hope to demonstrate in a future study, the PrabhÄsaká¹£etramÄhÄtmya displays numerous signs of representing a mid-twelfth-century redaction. It is intent upon, as it tells us, bringing existing traditions into alignment with the Vaidika norms (vedasammata). Yet at the same time, it has actively drawn upon a range of older Tantric sources, which are often only partially digested. This state of affairs explains the ubiquitous casual allusions throughout the text to Tantric trade secrets such as the thirty-six tattvas and the conquest of the six adhvans. Indeed, in its second chapter, which candidly alludes to some of the mÄhÄtmyaâ€™s sources, the work actually refers to many such kalpas by name, associated with MahÄlaká¹£mÄ«, the Garuá¸a Tantras, and so forth, aligning them with specific subsections within the text. Often the redactional process, which imposes a proto- SmÄrta-Åšaiva veneer, is extremely transparent. Consistently, what lies underneath, right down to the retention of aiÅ›a modes of compounding and idiom, (such as an eccentric use of x-vivarjita in the sense of transcending-x as opposed to devoid of x, resembling early KÄlÄ« Krama scriptures) is a heavily epistemologized and vaguely non- dual form of ÅšÄkta Tantra. Thus, SomeÅ›a himself, for example, in the sixth chapter, is identified in passing as originally being known of as NirvÄá¹‡abhairava, who is beyond duality (dvaitavarjitaá¹ƒ), whose state is devoid of the elaboration of language (vÄkprapaÃ±cÄdirahitam) and is established in the observation of knowledge and the means of knowing (jÃ±ÄnajÃ±eyÄvalokasthitaá¹ƒ) and yet beyond the mere appearance of causality (hetvÄbhÄsavivarjitam) (PrabhÄsaká¹£etramÄhÄtmya 5.10â€“11). The text discusses at some length how these Bhairavamata traditions have in the present generation been replaced by the recitation of the ÅšatarudrÄ«ya and other Vedicizing Åšaiva texts. As for some of the original demographics involved in making pilgrimage to the region, the third chapter in this regard is particularly telling. After first introducing the practice of pilgrimage in the most general terms, it then obsesses at some length with remarkable specificity about the â€œwrong motivationsâ€ and wrong understanding for going on pilgrimage, alluding, for example to a tradition of rasa siddhas whose activities are in opposition to vará¹‡ÄÅ›ramadharma. PrabhÄsaká¹£etramÄhÄtmya, pg. 5, vs. 3.119â€“122: evam uktaá¹ƒ mahÄdevi prabhÄsaá¹ƒ ká¹£etraá¹ƒ uttamaá¹ƒ / dá¹›á¹£á¹­vÄ saá¹ƒskÄrarahitÄá¸¥ kalau pÄpena mohitÄá¸¥ // rÄjasÄs tÄmasÄÅ› caiva pÄpohatacetasaá¸¥ / paradÄraparadravyaparahiá¹ƒsÄratÄ narÄá¸¥ // udvegaá¹ƒ ca paraá¹ƒ yÄti pratapyanti yas tataá¸¥ / Ätmasaá¹ƒbhÄvitÄ mÅ«á¸Ä mithyÄjÃ±Änena mohitÄá¸¥ // vará¹‡ÄÅ›ramaviruddhaá¹ƒ tu tÄ«rthaá¹ƒ kurvanti yeâ€™dhamÄá¸¥ / ↩︎

In actuality, Laká¹£mÄ«dharaâ€™s ÅšÄtÄtapasmá¹›ti shares its title but not content with the published work under that name, for the ÅšÄtÄtapasmá¹›ti available to us is utterly dissimilar in tone and focus. Much of the work consists simply of a charming laundry list of hyper specific â€œif, thenâ€ formulations, matching consequence to karmic result. To cite just a few compelling examples, a person who passes off someone elseâ€™s words as their own will be reborn dumb, and the remedy for this in a future life will be to give another scholar copies of works of NyÄya and ItihÄsa in manuscript. While a person who steals wool in this life will be reborn really hairy, a state of affairs that can be remedied by making a golden image of Agni and giving it to a BrÄhmaá¹‡a, in contrast, a person who steals silk will be reborn with an unmanly lack of hair, which is fixed for future births by giving a BrÄhmaá¹‡a a cow. If you have incestuous sex with your mother, next life you will be born with a vestigial penis. Sleeping with your maternal aunt causes ulcers on the left side, incest with your paternal aunt makes them manifest on the left. On and on the text goes, without at any point expressing even a passing interested in the examination and evaluation of different classes of BrÄhmaá¹‡as attested in the dharmanibandha literature. HemÄdri next begins to incorporate devotional theistic religion into this field of discourse. This will ultimately prove to be a rather involved and multistep process.
